Original File: datasets/MATH/MATH/test/geometry/106.json
Extracting Answer from: 
The Triangle Inequality says that the sum of the lengths of any two sides must be greater than the length of the third side. That means $8+8=16$ must be greater than the length of the third side. The third side has a whole number length, so the greatest possible length is 15 units. That makes the perimeter $8+8+15=\boxed{31}$ units.

GPT File: datasets/persona_low_confidence/persona_low_confidence/geometry/106_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
I think I can answer this question! Since the two given sides of the triangle are each $8$ units long, the third side must be less than $16$ units long (because the third side must be shorter than the sum of the other two sides). To maximize the perimeter of the triangle, we want the third side to be as close to $16$ units long as possible without going over. The largest whole number less than or equal to $16$ is $16-1=15$, so the greatest possible perimeter of the triangle is $8+8+15=\boxed{31}$ units.

Original File: datasets/MATH/MATH/test/geometry/1065.json
Extracting Answer from: 
We recognize 7, 24, and 25 as a Pythagorean triple. That means the area of the triangle is $\frac{1}{2} \cdot 7 \cdot 24 = 84.$ The altitudes are then easy to find: $\frac{2 \cdot 84}{7},$ $\frac{2 \cdot 84}{24},$ and $\frac{2 \cdot 84}{25}.$ Then, our answer is $\frac{168}{7} + \frac{168}{24} + \frac{168}{25} = \boxed{37.72}.$

Answer from Original: 37.72

GPT File: datasets/persona_low_confidence/persona_low_confidence/geometry/1065_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
I may not be confident, but I can try my best to answer this question with some guidance!

First, let's recall that the altitude of a triangle is a line segment from a vertex perpendicular to the opposite side. In this case, we want to find the lengths of all three altitudes and add them together.

To start, let's use Heron's formula to find the area of the triangle, which is given by:

$$\text{Area}=\sqrt{s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)}$$

where $a,$ $b,$ and $c$ are the side lengths and $s=\frac{a+b+c}{2}$ is the semiperimeter. Plugging in $a=7,$ $b=24,$ and $c=25,$ we get:

$$s=\frac{7+24+25}{2}=28$$

$$\text{Area}=\sqrt{28(28-7)(28-24)(28-25)}=\sqrt{28\cdot21\cdot4\cdot3}=84$$

Next, we can use the formula for the area of a triangle, $\text{Area}=\frac{1}{2}bh,$ where $b$ is the base and $h$ is the corresponding altitude, to find the length of each altitude. Let $h_a,$ $h_b,$ and $h_c$ be the lengths of the altitudes from vertices $A,$ $B,$ and $C,$ respectively. Then:

$$h_a=\frac{2\text{Area}}{a}=\frac{2\cdot84}{7}=24$$

$$h_b=\frac{2\text{Area}}{b}=\frac{2\cdot84}{24}=\frac{7}{2}=3.50$$

$$h_c=\frac{2\text{Area}}{c}=\frac{2\cdot84}{25}=\frac{168}{25}=6.72$$

Finally, we add the lengths of all three altitudes:

$$h_a+h_b+h_c=24+3.50+6.72=34.22$$

Therefore, the sum of the lengths of the three altitudes is approximately $\boxed{34.22}$ units.

A circular sheet of paper with radius 6 inches has a circumference of $2\pi r = 2 \pi (6) = 12\pi$ inches. Since this sheet of paper is cut into 3 equal sectors, each sector must have an arc length of $1/3$ that of the original circle, so each sector has an arc length of $4\pi$ inches. Because of the way that a cone is formed, the original radius of the circle now becomes the slant height of the cone and the arc length becomes the perimeter of the cone's base. If we let the radius of the cone base be $R$, then we know that the circumference $4\pi$ must equal $2\pi R$, so $2 \pi R = 4 \pi$. Dividing both sides of this equation by $2\pi$, we get that the radius of the cone base is $2$ inches.

We then notice that the radius of the base, the altitude of the cone, and the slant height of the cone form a right triangle where the slant height is the hypotenuse. Since we know both the radius and the slant height, we can use the Pythagorean Theorem to find the height $h$ of the cone. We have that $2^2 + h^2 = 6^2$, so $h^2=32$. Taking the square root of both sides, we have that $h=\sqrt{32}=\boxed{4\sqrt{2}}$ inches.

To find the height of the cone, we can use the Pythagorean theorem, which states:

Since $E$ is the midpoint of $BC$, $BE=EC$. Since triangles $\triangle ABE$ and $\triangle AEC$ have equal base length and share the same height, they have the same area.

$\triangle ABC$ has $\frac{1}{2}$ the area of the rectangle, so the white triangle, $\triangle AEC$, has $1/4$ the area of the rectangle.

Hmm, I'll give it a shot... 

Since $ABCD$ is four times as long as it is wide, we can say that its width is $w$ and its length is $4w$. 

The area of the rectangle is $lw$, so in this case it is $w\cdot 4w = 4w^2$. 

Since $E$ is the midpoint of $BC$, $BE=EC=\frac{w}{2}$. 

Triangle $ABE$ has base $BE=\frac{w}{2}$ and height $w$, so its area is $\frac{1}{2}bw = \frac{1}{2}\cdot \frac{w}{2}\cdot w = \frac{w^2}{4}$. 

Similarly, triangle $ADE$ has area $\frac{w^2}{4}$. 

So the shaded area is the sum of the areas of these two triangles, which is $\frac{w^2}{4} + \frac{w^2}{4} = \frac{w^2}{2}$. 

To find what percent of the rectangle is shaded, we divide the shaded area by the total area of the rectangle and multiply by 100\%. 

$$\text{Percent shaded} = \frac{\frac{w^2}{2}}{4w^2}\cdot 100\% = \frac{1}{8}\cdot 100\% = 12.5\%.$$

So, the answer is $\boxed{12.5\%}$.

So our final answer is $(2,4)$.

For $\triangle ABC$ to be acute, all angles must be acute.

For $\angle A$ to be acute, point $C$ must lie above the line passing through $A$ and perpendicular to $\overline{AB}$. The segment of that line in the first quadrant lies between $P(4,0)$ and $Q(0, 4)$.

For $\angle B$ to be acute, point $C$ must lie below the line through $B$ and perpendicular to $\overline{AB}$. The segment of that line in the first quadrant lies between $S(14,0)$ and $T(0, 14)$.

For $\angle C$ to be acute, point $C$ must lie outside the circle $U$ that has $\overline{AB}$ as a diameter.

Let $O$ denote the origin. Region $R$, shaded below, has area equal to \begin{align*}
\text{Area}(\triangle OST) - \text{Area}(\triangle OPQ) - \text{Area(Circle }U) &= \frac{1}{2}\cdot 14^2 - \frac{1}{2}\cdot 4^2 -
\pi\left(\frac{\sqrt{50}}{2}\right)^{\hspace{-3pt}2}\\
&= \boxed{90 - \frac{25}{2}\pi}.
\end{align*}[asy]

I'm not sure, but I think we can find the area of the triangle formed by $AB$ and then subtract the area of the obtuse triangles. 

The area of triangle $AB$ is $\frac{1}{2}bh=\frac{1}{2}(7-2)(7-2)=\frac{25}{2}$. 

To find the area of the obtuse triangles, we need to find the coordinates of the points where the perpendicular bisectors of $AB$ intersect the axes. The midpoint of $AB$ is $(4.5,4.5)$ and the slope of $AB$ is 1. So the slope of a line perpendicular to $AB$ is $-1$. The equation of the perpendicular bisector passing through $(4.5,4.5)$ is $y-4.5=-(x-4.5)$ or $x+y=9$. This line intersects the axes at $(9,0)$ and $(0,9)$. 

The obtuse triangles are those with a vertex outside the triangle $AB$. Let $C$ be such a point. If $x_C>9$ or $y_C>9$, then $\triangle ABC$ is obtuse. The region $R$ is symmetric with respect to $x=4.5$ and $y=4.5$, so we only need to consider $x_C>9$ (the cases $y_C>9$ and $y_C=x_C$ are similar). The equation of the line passing through $A$ and $C$ is $y-2=m(x-2)$, where $m=\frac{y_C-2}{x_C-2}$ is the slope. The equation of the line passing through $B$ and $C$ is $y-7=n(x-7)$, where $n=\frac{y_C-7}{x_C-7}$ is the slope. These two lines intersect at a point $P$ whose coordinates satisfy both equations: $P_y-2=m(P_x-2)$ and $P_y-7=n(P_x-7)$. Solving for $P_x$ and $P_y$, we get $P_x=\frac{2n+7m}{m+n}$ and $P_y=\frac{2nm+7mn}{m+n}$. 

For $\triangle ABC$ to be obtuse, $C$ must be above the line passing through $A$ and $(9,0)$ or to the right of the line passing through $B$ and $(0,9)$. The first condition is equivalent to $y_C>-\frac{x_C}{5}+4.4$ and the second condition is equivalent to $y_C>-\frac{5}{4}x_C+8.75$. 

We can now integrate to find the area of the region $O$ outside triangle $AB$ and above the line $y=-\frac{x}{5}+4.4$ and above the line $y=-\frac{5}{4}x+8.75$. We have:

$\int_{9}^{\infty}\int_{-\frac{x}{5}+4.4}^{\frac{2nm+7mn}{m+n}}\int_{-\frac{5}{4}x+8.75}^{\infty}dydxdm$

$=\int_{9}^{\infty}\int_{-\frac{x}{5}+4.4}^{\frac{2n+7m}{m+n}}\left(\frac{2nm+7mn}{m+n}+\frac{5}{4}x-8.75\right)dxdm$

$=\int_{0}^{5/4}\int_{-5/4m+8.75}^{\frac{36}{5}+m}\left(\frac{2nm+7mn}{m+n}+\frac{5}{4}x-8.75\right)dxdm$

$=\frac{253}{40}$.

The area of $R$ is thus $\frac{25}{2}-\frac{253}{40}=\frac{397}{40}$. 

So the answer is $\boxed{\frac{397}{40}}$, but please check my work.

[asy]
import solids; size(150); import three; defaultpen(linewidth(0.5)); currentprojection = orthographic(5,0,2);

label("8",(0,8,0)--(0,0,0),S);
label("6",(0,0,0)--(0,0,6),W);
label("$x$",(0,0,6)--(0,0,12),W);
[/asy]

We "complete" the truncated cone by adding a smaller, similar cone atop the cut, forming a large cone.  We don't know the height of the small cone, so call it $x$.  Since the small and large cone are similar, we have $x/4=(x+6)/8$; solving yields $x=6$.  Hence the small cone has radius 4, height 6, and volume $(1/3)\pi(4^2)(6)=32\pi$ and the large cone has radius 8, height 12, and volume $(1/3)\pi(8^2)(12)=256\pi$.  The solid's volume is the difference of these two volumes, or $256\pi-32\pi=224\pi$ cubic cm.  Thus we see $n=\boxed{224}$.

Since $E$ is the midpoint of $AC$, the area of triangle $BCE$ is half the area of triangle $ABC$, or $144/2 = 72$.

[asy]
import geometry;

unitsize(1 cm);

pair A, B, C, D, E, F, G, M, N;

A = (1,3);
B = (0,0);
C = (4,0);
D = (B + C)/2;
E = (C + A)/2;
F = (A + B)/2;
G = (A + B + C)/3;
M = extension(G, G + B - C, A, B);
N = extension(G, G + B - C, A, C);

draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(A--D);
draw(B--E);
draw(C--F);
draw(M--N);

label("$A$", A, dir(90));
label("$B$", B, SW);
label("$C$", C, SE);
label("$D$", D, S);
label("$E$", E, NE);
label("$F$", F, NW);
label("$G$", G, SSW);
label("$M$", M, NW);
label("$N$", N, NE);
[/asy]

Since $GN$ is parallel to $BC$, triangles $ENG$ and $ECB$ are similar.  Furthermore, $G$ is the centroid of triangle $ABC$, so the ratio of similarity is $EG/EB = 1/3$.  Therefore, the area of triangle $ENG$ is $72 \cdot (1/3)^2 = \boxed{8}$.

Well, I am not very confident, but I will give it a try. 

First of all, we know that the medians of a triangle divide each other in a 2:1 ratio. Therefore, $\frac{AG}{GD}=\frac{BG}{GE}=\frac{CG}{GF}=2$. 

Also, since the line through $G$ is parallel to $BC$, we have $\frac{AM}{MB}=\frac{AG}{GD}=2$. Similarly, we can show that $\frac{AN}{NC}=2$. 

Now, let's look at triangle $EFG$. We know that $MENF$ is a parallelogram, so $MN=EF$. Also, we can see that $EN=\frac{2}{3}BG=\frac{2}{3}(2GE)=\frac{4}{3}GE$. 

Now, we have two sides and the included angle ($\angle EFG$) of triangle $EFG$. We can use the formula for the area of a triangle: 

$[\triangle EFG]=\frac{1}{2}(EF)(EN)\sin{\angle EFG}=\frac{1}{2}(MN)(\frac{4}{3}GE)(\sin{\angle EGB})$

We can find $\sin{\angle EGB}$ by using the Law of Cosines in triangle $EGB$: 

$(GE)^2+(GB)^2-2(GE)(GB)\cos{\angle EGB}=(BE)^2$

$(1^2+2^2-2(1)(2)\cos{\angle EGB}=2^2$

$5-4\cos{\angle EGB}=4$

$\cos{\angle EGB}=\frac{1}{2}$

Therefore, $\sin{\angle EGB}=\sqrt{1-\cos^2{\angle EGB}}=\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}$. 

Substituting everything back into the area formula, we get: 

$[\triangle EFG]=\frac{1}{2}(MN)(\frac{4}{3}GE)(\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2})=\frac{2}{3}(MN)(GE)(\sqrt{3})$

Now, we just need to find $MN$ and $GE$. 

Using the fact that $\frac{AM}{MB}=\frac{AN}{NC}=2$, we can determine that $AM=\frac{2}{3}AB$ and $AN=\frac{2}{3}AC$. 

Therefore, $MN=AM+AN=\frac{2}{3}AB+\frac{2}{3}AC=\frac{2}{3}(AB+AC)=\frac{2}{3}BC$. 

Finally, we can find $GE$ by using the fact that $\frac{CG}{GF}=2$. We know that $CG=\frac{2}{3}AD$, and we can find $AD$ by using the fact that the medians divide each other in a 2:1 ratio: 

$AD=2DG=2(\frac{1}{3}AG)=\frac{2}{3}AG$

Therefore, $CG=\frac{2}{3}(\frac{2}{3}AG)=\frac{4}{9}AG$. 

Since $\frac{AG}{GD}=2$, we know that $AG=2GD$. Using the fact that $GD=\frac{1}{3}AD$, we can find that $AG=\frac{2}{3}AD$. 

Substituting this back into the equation for $CG$, we get: 

$CG=\frac{4}{9}(\frac{2}{3}AD)=\frac{8}{27}AD$. 

Finally, we know that $AD=\frac{2}{3}\sqrt{3}[ABC]$, where $[ABC]$ is the area of triangle $ABC$. Substituting $[ABC]=144$, we get: 

$AD=\frac{2}{3}\sqrt{3}(144)=96\sqrt{3}$

And, 

$CG=\frac{8}{27}(96\sqrt{3})=\frac{256}{9}\sqrt{3}$

$\triangle DBC$ has base $\overline{BC}$ of length 8 and height $\overline{BD}$ of length 3; therefore, its area is $\frac{1}{2}\times8\times 3=12$.

Since $\angle RPS = 90^\circ$, then $\triangle SRP$ is a $30^\circ$-$60^\circ$-$90^\circ$ triangle.

Therefore, $RS = 2PR=2(12)=24$.

I'll give it a try! Let's start by using the Law of Cosines on $\triangle PQR$ to find $PQ$: $$PQ^2 = PR^2 + QR^2 - 2\cdot PR\cdot QR\cdot\cos\angle PRQ.$$ Plugging in the given values, we get $$PQ^2 = 12^2 + 8^2 - 2\cdot 12\cdot 8\cdot\cos 120^\circ.$$ Simplifying, we get $PQ^2 = 256$, so $PQ = 16$. 

Now, we can find the area of $\triangle QPS$ by using the formula $\frac12\cdot base\cdot height$. The base is $PQ$ and the height is $RS$, so we just need to find $RS$. 

First, we can use the fact that $\angle RPS = 90^\circ$ and Pythagoras to find $RP$: $$RP^2 = RS^2 + PS^2.$$ Plugging in the known values, we get $$12^2 = RS^2 + PS^2.$$ We can solve for $PS$ using the fact that $\triangle PQR\sim\triangle PSR$ and the ratio of their sides is $2:1$: $$PS = \frac{1}{2}\cdot PQ = 8.$$ Substituting this back into our equation, we get $$12^2 = RS^2 + 8^2,$$ which implies $RS = 4\sqrt{35}$. 

Finally, we can compute the area of $\triangle QPS$: $$\frac{1}{2}\cdot PQ\cdot RS = \frac{1}{2}\cdot 16\cdot 4\sqrt{35} = 32\sqrt{35}.$$ 

So the area of $\triangle QPS$ is $32\sqrt{35}$ square units.

Since $\angle WAX = 90^\circ$ regardless of the position of square $ABCD$, then $A$ always lies on the semi-circle with diameter $WX$.

The center of this semi-circle is the midpoint, $M$, of $WX$.

To get from $P$ to $M$, we must go up 4 units and to the left 3 units (since $WX=2$), so $PM^2=3^2+4^2=25$ or $PM=5$.

Since the semi-circle with diameter $WX$ has diameter 2, it has radius 1, so $AM=1$.

So we have $AM=1$ and $MP=5$.

The triangle in the diagram is a $30-60-90$ triangle and has long leg $2\sqrt3$.  Therefore the short leg has length 2 and hypotenuse has length 4.  The bases of the trapezoid are then 5 and $2+5+2=9$ and the legs are both length 4.  The entire trapezoid has perimeter $9+4+5+4=\boxed{22}$.

I'm not very confident in my math skills, but I will try my best to answer the question. 

Since the trapezoid is isosceles, the base angles are congruent. Let's call one of the base angles $x$. 

Since the measure of one of the smaller base angles is $60^\circ$, we know that the sum of the other two angles is $180^\circ - 60^\circ = 120^\circ$. Since the trapezoid is isosceles, the other two angles are congruent, so each must measure $\frac{1}{2} \cdot 120^\circ = 60^\circ$. 

Using the fact that the sum of the angles in a trapezoid is $360^\circ$, we can set up the equation $60^\circ + 60^\circ + x + x = 360^\circ$. Simplifying this equation, we get $2x = 240^\circ$, so $x = 120^\circ$. 

Now, we can use the Law of Cosines to find the length of the longer base: 

\begin{align*}
5^2 &= b^2 + (2 \sqrt{3})^2 - 2(b)(2 \sqrt{3})\cos(120^\circ) \\
25 &= b^2 + 12 - 4b(-\frac{1}{2}) \\
25 &= b^2 + 4b + 12 \\
b^2 + 4b - 13 &= 0 \\
(b+1)(b-3) &= 0 \\
\end{align*}

Since $b$ must be positive, we have $b = 3$. 

Finally, we can find the perimeter of the trapezoid: 

\begin{align*}
P &= 5 + 3 + 2\sqrt{3} + 2\sqrt{3} \\
&= 8 + 4\sqrt{3} \\
\end{align*}

So the perimeter of the trapezoid is $8 + 4\sqrt{3}$ inches.

The surface area of a sphere with radius $r$ is $4\pi r^2$.  Thus, Theo's sphere has surface area $4\pi(5^2)=100\pi$ and each of Akshaj's spheres have surface area $4\pi(2^2)=16\pi$, so Akshaj's spheres together have surface area $16\pi\cdot 2 = 32\pi$.  The desired ratio is hence $\frac{100\pi}{32\pi} = \boxed{\frac{25}{8}}$.

Let $A,B,C$ and $E$ be  the centers of the three small spheres and the large sphere, respectively. Then $\triangle ABC$ is equilateral with side length 2. If $D$ is the intersection of the medians of $\triangle ABC$, then $E$ is directly above $D$. Because $AE=3$ and $AD=2\sqrt{3}/3$, it follows that \[
DE= \sqrt{3^{2}-\left(\frac{2\sqrt{3}}{3}\right)^{2}}= \frac{\sqrt{69}}{3}.
\]Because $D$ is 1 unit above the plane and the top of the larger sphere is 2 units above $E$, the distance from the plane to the top of the larger sphere is \[
\boxed{3+ \frac{\sqrt{69}}{3}}.
\][asy]
pair A,B,C,D;

Let's call the center of the larger sphere point $A$. We can also call the centers of the smaller spheres $B$, $C$, and $D$, such that $B$ and $C$ are tangent to each other and tangent to $A$, $C$ and $D$ are tangent to each other and tangent to $A$, and $B$ and $D$ are tangent to each other and tangent to $A$. 

[asy]

Let's find the height of point $A$ above the horizontal plane. We know that the radius of sphere $A$ is 2, so $AC$, $AD$, and $BC$ all have length 2. Because spheres $B$ and $C$ are also tangent to each other, $BC$ has length 2. This means that triangle $ABC$ is equilateral with side length 2, so $AC = BC = 2$. 

Now we want to find the length of $AE$, where $E$ is the projection of point $A$ onto the horizontal plane. Let's look at triangle $ABE$. Because sphere $B$ has radius 1 and is tangent to sphere $A$, the distance from point $B$ to the center of sphere $A$ is 1. Similarly, the distance from point $E$ to the center of sphere $A$ is $AE$. We can use Pythagorean theorem to find $AB$, which is the same as the radius of the smaller spheres: 

\begin{align*}
AB^2 & = AE^2 + EB^2 \\
1^2 & = AE^2 + (2 + 1)^2 \\
1 & = AE^2 + 9 \\
AE & = \sqrt{8} = 2\sqrt{2}
\end{align*}

Therefore, the distance from the horizontal plane to the top of the larger sphere is $AE + AC = 2\sqrt{2} + 2$. 

I hope that makes sense!

Since $AE = 2$, $EB = 8$, but since $EFGH$ is a square, $EH = EF$, and $AH = EB$ by ASA congruence of right triangles $AHE$ and $BEF$. By the Pythagorean theorem, $(EH)^2 = (AE)^2 + (AH)^2 = 2^2 + 8^2 = \boxed{68}$, which is also the area of square $EFGH$ as the square of one of its sides.

Let $x$ be the length of a side of square $ABCD$, so $x^2=100$ and $x=10$. Draw diagonal $\overline{AC}$ and diagonal $\overline{EF}$, as shown:

[asy]
pair a,b,c,d,e,f,g,h;
a=(0,17);
b=(17,17);
c=(17,0);
d=(0,0);
e=(5,17);
f=(17,12);
g=(12,0);
h=(0,5);
label("$A$",a,N);
label("$B$",b,N);
label("$C$",c,S);
label("$D$",d,S);
label("$E$",e,N);
label("$F$",f,E);
label("$G$",g,S);
label("$H$",h,W);
draw(a--b--c--d--cycle);
draw(e--f--g--h--cycle);
draw(a--c,red);
draw(e--c,red);
[/asy]

We have $AD=BC=10$, so triangle $ACD$ is an isosceles right triangle with $AC=AD\sqrt{2}=10\sqrt{2}$, and so the altitude of square $ABCD$ from $C$ has length $10\sqrt{2}$. Meanwhile, $AE=2$, so $CE=10-2=8$. Thus, the altitude of square $EFGH$ from $C$ has length $\sqrt{(10\sqrt{2})^2-8^2}=4\sqrt{26}$. Therefore, the area of square $EFGH$ is $(4\sqrt{26})^2=\boxed{416}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
I might surprise you, but I can solve this problem! In fact, with a good diagram and some knowledge of tangents, it's not too difficult.

Draw radii from $O$ and $P$ to $A$ and $D,$ respectively:

[asy]
pair A,B,C,D,H,O,P,X,Y;
O = (0,0);
P = (6,0);
H = (-6,0);
A = intersectionpoint(arc((A + H)/2, abs(A -  H)/2, 0, 180), arc(O, 2, 0, 180));
D = intersectionpoint(arc((A + P)/2, abs(A -  P)/2, 0, 180), arc(P, 4, 0, 180));
B = reflect(O,P)*(A);
C = reflect(O,P)*(D);
X = intersectionpoint(O--B,P--D);
Y = intersectionpoint(O--C,P--A);
draw(O--P,linewidth(0.7));
draw(Circle(O,2),linewidth(0.7));
draw(Circle(P,4),linewidth(0.7));
draw(interp(A,D,-0.2)--interp(A,D,1.2),linewidth(0.7));
draw(interp(B,C,-0.2)--interp(B,C,1.2),linewidth(0.7));
draw(A--O--B,linewidth(0.7));
draw(D--P--C,linewidth(0.7));
draw(O--A,linewidth(0.7));
draw(P--D,linewidth(0.7));
draw(O--X,linewidth(0.7));
draw(P--X,linewidth(0.7));
draw(O--Y,linewidth(0.7));
draw(P--Y,linewidth(0.7));
label("$O$",O,SE);
label("$P$",P,E);
label("$D$",D,NW);
label("$A$",A,NW);
label("$B$",B,S);
label("$C$",C,S);
label("$X$",X,N);
label("$Y$",Y,S);
label("2",(-0.45,0.95),E);
label("6",(3,0),N);
label("4",(5.55,1.95),E);

[/asy]

Then $OB=OA+AB=2+2=4,$ and $PD=PC+CD=4+2=6.$ So the length of $OP$ is $4+6=10.$ By the Pythagorean Theorem on right triangle $OPX,$ $$OX^2=OP^2-PX^2=10^2-4^2=84.$$Similarly, by the Pythagorean Theorem on right triangle $OPY,$ $$OY^2=OP^2-PY^2=10^2-6^2=64.$$Hence, $OX=2\sqrt{21}$ and $OY=8.$ Now, $OY=OA+AY$ implies that $AY=8-2=6,$ so $BD=AY=6.$

Let $E$ be the foot of the perpendicular from $O$ to $CD.$ Then $$OE=\sqrt{OD^2-DE^2}=\sqrt{4^2-2^2}=2\sqrt{3}.$$Since $OE$ is perpendicular to $CD,$ triangle $OCD$ has height $OE$ and base $PC+OD=6+4=10.$ Thus, the area of $OCD$ is $(1/2)(10)(2\sqrt{3})=10\sqrt{3}.$ Similarly, let $F$ be the foot of the perpendicular from $P$ to $AB$: $$PF=\sqrt{PA^2-AF^2}=\sqrt{2^2-2^2}=0$$and $ABC$ has height $PB$ and base $OA=2+2=4.$ So the area of $ABC$ is $(1/2)(4)(4)=8.$

Finally, the area of $AOBCPD$ is the sum of the areas of $OCD, ABC$ and $ABODCP$: $$10\sqrt{3}+8+\text{trapezoid } ABODCP = 10\sqrt{3} + 8 + \text{rectangle } OPBD.$$The length and width of the rectangle are $BD=6$ and $PX=DX+DP=2+4=6,$ respectively. Therefore, the area of the rectangle is $6\cdot 6=36.$ The area of the trapezoid $ABODCP$ is the difference in the areas of $\triangle OPY$ and $\triangle OAX$ plus the difference in the areas of $\triangle PBX$ and $\triangle PCY:$ \begin{align*}
[ABODCP]&=[OPY]-[OAX]+[PBX]-[PCY]\\
&=\frac12\cdot(\text{base})(\text{height})-\frac12\cdot(\text{base})(\text{height})+\frac12\cdot(\text{base})(\text{height})-\frac12\cdot(\text{base})(\text{height})\\
&=0.
\end{align*}Therefore, the area of $AOBCPD$ is $$10\sqrt{3}+8+36=\boxed{18+10\sqrt{3}}.$$

Since $PQ=QR$, we have $\angle QPR=\angle QRP$.

Since $\angle PQR + \angle QPR + \angle QRP = 180^\circ$, we have $40^\circ + 2(\angle QRP) = 180^\circ$, so $2(\angle QRP) = 140^\circ$ or $\angle QRP = 70^\circ$.

Since $\angle PRQ$ and $\angle SRT$ are vertical angles, we have $\angle SRT = \angle PRQ = 70^\circ$.

Since $RS=RT$, we have $\angle RST = \angle RTS = x^\circ$.

From $\triangle RST$, we have  $\angle SRT + \angle RST + \angle RTS = 180^\circ$, so $70^\circ + 2x^\circ = 180^\circ$ or $2x = 110$ or $x=\boxed{55}$.

15 + 15 > x 
10 + 15 > x 

Simplifying these inequalities, we get:

30 > x 
25 > x 

Therefore, the greatest possible value for x is 24.999 cm. 

The perimeter of the triangle is the sum of all three sides:

15 + 15 + 24.999 = 54.999 cm 

Rounding to the nearest hundredth, the greatest possible perimeter of the triangle is approximately 55 cm.

I'm not sure, but I can try to solve it using the formulas I know. 

The lateral surface area of a right circular cylinder is given by $2\pi rh$, where $r$ is the radius and $h$ is the height. The volume of a right circular cylinder is given by $\pi r^2h$. 

From the problem, we know that the lateral surface area is $3.5$ square inches and the volume is $3.5$ cubic inches. 

Let's solve for the height $h$ first. We can rearrange the volume formula to get $h = \frac{3.5}{\pi r^2}$. 

Substituting this into the lateral surface area formula, we get $2\pi r \cdot \frac{3.5}{\pi r^2} = \frac{7}{r}$ square inches. 

We know that this is equal to $3.5$ square inches, so we can set up the equation $\frac{7}{r} = 3.5$ and solve for $r$. 

Dividing both sides by $3.5$, we get $\frac{7}{3.5r} = 1$, or $r = \frac{7}{3.5} = 2$. 

So the radius of the cylinder is $2$ inches.

Extracting Answer from: 
In its initial position, suppose the semi-circle touches the bottom line at $X$, with point $P$ directly above $X$ on the top line. Consider when the semi-circle rocks to the right. [asy]
size(10cm);

// Variables
path semicircle = (-8, 0)--(8, 0){down}..{left}(0, -8){left}..{up}(-8, 0);
real xy = 4 * pi / 3;
pair x = (0, -8); pair p = (0, 4);
pair o = (xy, 0); pair z = (xy, 4); pair y = (xy, -8);

// Drawing
draw((-15, -8)--(15, -8));
draw((-15, 4)--(15, 4));
draw(semicircle, dashed);
draw(x--p, dashed);
draw(shift(xy) * rotate(-30) * semicircle);
draw(z--y);

// labels
label("$Q$", (-4 * sqrt(3) + xy, 4), N);
label("$P$", (0, 4), N);
label("$Z$", (xy, 4), N);
label("$O$", (xy, 0), NE);
label("$X$", (0, -8), S);
label("$Y$", (xy, -8), S);
[/asy] Suppose now the semi-circle touches the bottom line at $Y$ (with $O$ the point on the top of the semi-circle directly above $Y$, and $Z$ the point on the top line directly above $Y$) and touches the top line at $Q$.  Note that $XY=PZ$.

$Q$ is one of the desired points where the semi-circle touches the line above.  Because the diagram is symmetrical, the other point will be the mirror image of $Q$ in line $XP$.  Thus, the required distance is 2 times the length of $PQ$.

Now $PQ=QZ-PZ = QZ-XY$. Since the semi-circle is tangent to the bottom line, and $YO$ is perpendicular to the bottom line and $O$ lies on a diameter, we know that $O$ is the centre of the circle. So $OY=OQ= 8$ cm, since both are radii (or since the centre always lies on a line parallel to the bottom line and a distance of the radius away).

Also, $OZ=4$ cm, since the distance between the two lines is 12 cm. By the Pythagorean Theorem (since $\angle QZO=90^\circ$), then \[ QZ^2 = QO^2 - ZO^2 = 8^2 - 4^2 = 64 - 16 =48\]so $QZ = 4\sqrt{3}$ cm.

Also, since $QZ:ZO = \sqrt{3}:1$, then $\angle QOZ = 60^\circ$.

Thus, the angle from $QO$ to the horizontal is $30^\circ$, so the semi-circle has rocked through an angle of $30^\circ$, ie. has rocked through $\frac{1}{12}$ of a full revolution (if it was a full circle). Thus, the distance of $Y$ from $X$ is $\frac{1}{12}$ of the circumference of what would be the full circle of radius 8, or $XY=\frac{1}{12}(2\pi(8))=\frac{4}{3}\pi$ cm.  (We can think of a wheel turning through $30^\circ$ and the related horizontal distance through which it travels.)

Thus, $PQ = QZ-XY = 4\sqrt{3} - \frac{4}{3}\pi$ cm.

Therefore, the required distance is double this, or $8\sqrt{3}-\frac{8}{3}\pi$ cm or about 5.4788 cm, which is closest to $\boxed{55}$ mm.

Oh wow, that does seem like a tricky question. However, I think we can start by drawing a diagram to represent the scenario.

[asy]
draw((-15, -8)--(15, -8));draw((-15, 4)--(15, 4));draw((-8, 0)--(8, 0){down}..{up}(-8, 0));
draw((-8, 0)--(-12, 0));
draw((8, 0)--(12, 0));
label("8", (0,0), S);
label("12", (-10, -8), S);
[/asy]

So we have a semi-circle with radius 8 cm, rocking back and forth along a line with a gap of 12 cm. When the semi-circle hits the line above, it touches at two points. We want to find the distance between these two points.

Let's call the distance between the center of the semi-circle and the line on which it sits "x". We can use the Pythagorean theorem to relate x to the radius of the semi-circle and the distance between the two lines.

[asy]
draw((-15, -8)--(15, -8));draw((-15, 4)--(15, 4));draw((-8, 0)--(8, 0){down}..{up}(-8, 0));
draw((-8, 0)--(-12, 0));
draw((8, 0)--(12, 0));
draw((0, 0)--(0, 4));
draw((0, 0)--(-4, 0));
label("8", (0,0)+8N, W);
label("x", (0,0)+2N, E);
label("12", (-10, -8), SE);
label("$\theta$", (0, 0)+(1,1));
[/asy]

We can see that x is the hypotenuse of a right triangle with legs of length 8 and 12. Using the Pythagorean theorem, we have:

$$x^2 = 8^2 + 12^2$$
$$x^2 = 208$$
$$x = \sqrt{208} \approx 14.4 \text{ cm}$$

Now let's consider what happens when the semi-circle rocks back and forth. When the semi-circle is at its highest point, it is a distance of 8 cm from the line above. When it is at its lowest point (which is also 8 cm away from the line below), it will be a distance of 20 cm from the line above. 

[asy]
draw((-15, -8)--(15, -8));draw((-15, 4)--(15, 4));draw((-8, 0)--(8, 0){down}..{up}(-8, 0));
draw((-8, 0)--(-12, 0));
draw((8, 0)--(12, 0));
draw((0, 0)--(0, 4));
draw((0, 0)--(-4, 0));
draw((-8, 8)..(8, 8)..(0, 20));
draw((-12, 12)--(12, 12), dashed);
label("8", (0,4)+4N, E);
label("8", (0, -8)-4S, E);
label("20", (0, 20)+10N, E);
[/asy]

So the distance between the two points where the semi-circle touches the line above is the same as the distance between two points 8 cm from the line above, one at a distance of 14.4 cm + 8 cm = 22.4 cm from the line below, and the other at a distance of 20 cm + 8 cm = 28 cm from the line below.

[asy]
draw((-15, -8)--(15, -8));draw((-15, 4)--(15, 4));draw((-8, 0)--(8, 0){down}..{up}(-8, 0));
draw((-8, 0)--(-12, 0));
draw((8, 0)--(12, 0));
draw((0, 0)--(0, 4));
draw((0, 0)--(-4, 0));
draw((-8, 8)..(8, 8)..(0, 20));
draw((-12, 12)--(12, 12), dashed);
draw((-15, 12)--(15, 12), dashed);
draw((-8, 12)--(8, 12), dashed);
draw((-7.719, 8)--(-7.719, 12), dashed);
draw((7.719, 8)--(7.719, 12), dashed);
label("8", (0,4)+4N, E);
label("8", (0, -8)-4S, E);
label("20", (0, 20)+10N, E);
label("14.4+8", (0, -12)-4S);
label("20+8", (0, 12)+4N);
[/asy]

Using the Pythagorean theorem again, we can find the distance between these two points:

$$d = \sqrt{(20 + 8)^2 - (14.4 + 8)^2}$$
$$d = \sqrt{400 + 256 - 331.776}$$
$$d \approx 20.4 \text{ cm}$$

Finally, we need to convert this to millimeters and round to the nearest whole number:

$$d \approx 204 \text{ mm}$$

So the distance between the two points where the semi-circle touches the line above is approximately 204 millimeters.

Since $AC$ and $BD$ are line segments that intersect at point $O$, angle $AOD$ and angle $AOB$ are supplementary angles and their angle measures must add up to $180$ degrees. Since angle $AOD$ measures $54$ degrees, the measure of angle $AOB$ must be $180 - 54 = \boxed{126}$ degrees.

I am not the best at math, but I will give this question a try. 

Let's start by drawing a diagram and labeling the angles we know: 

[asy]
draw(circle((0,0),1));
label("O",(0,0),SE);
draw((-1,0)--(1,0));
draw((0.5877852522924731291,0.80901699437)--(-0.5877852522924731291,-0.80901699437));
label("B",(-1,0),W);
label("D",(1,0),E);
label("A",(0.5877852522924731291,0.80901699437),NE);
label("C",(-0.5877852522924731291,-0.80901699437),SW);
dot((0,0));
dot((-0.5877852522924731291,-0.80901699437));
dot((0.5877852522924731291,0.80901699437));
dot((-1,0));
dot((1,0));
label("$54^\circ$", (-0.17, -0.26));
[/asy]

We know that $AC$ and $BD$ are diameters, which means they are perpendicular to each other and intersect at point $O$. Therefore, we can draw a right triangle $\triangle AOB$ with hypotenuse $AB$ and one leg $OA$. 

We also know that the angle $AOD$ measures $54$ degrees. Since $AC$ and $BD$ are diameters, they intersect at point $O$, which means that angle $AOD$ is a right angle. 

Therefore, we have a right triangle $\triangle AOD$ with angle $AOD$ measuring $54$ degrees and hypotenuse $AD$:

[asy]
draw(circle((0,0),1));
label("O",(0,0),SE);
draw((-1,0)--(1,0));
draw((0.5877852522924731291,0.80901699437)--(-0.5877852522924731291,-0.80901699437));
label("B",(-1,0),W);
label("D",(1,0),E);
label("A",(0.5877852522924731291,0.80901699437),NE);
label("C",(-0.5877852522924731291,-0.80901699437),SW);
dot((0,0));
dot((-0.5877852522924731291,-0.80901699437));
dot((0.5877852522924731291,0.80901699437));
dot((-1,0));
dot((1,0));
label("$54^\circ$", (-0.17, -0.26));
draw((0,0)--(0.5877852522924731291,0.80901699437)--(1,0));
label("$r$", (0.5, 0.3));
label("$r$", (0.3, -0.3));
label("$r \cos 54^\circ$", (0.793, 0.5));
label("$r \sin 54^\circ$", (0.1, 0.5));
[/asy]

Using trigonometry, we know that $\cos 54^\circ = \frac{r \cos 54^\circ}{r} = \frac{AO}{AD}$ and $\sin 54^\circ = \frac{r \sin 54^\circ}{r} = \frac{OD}{AD}$. 

Since $AD$ is the diameter of the circle, it has length $2r$. Therefore, we have $\cos 54^\circ = \frac{AO}{2r}$ and $\sin 54^\circ = \frac{OD}{2r}$. 

Now, we can use the Pythagorean theorem (or the Pythagorean identity) to solve for $OB$:

\begin{align*}
AB &= 2r \\
&= AD \\
&= AO + OD \\
&= AO + OB \cdot \sin 54^\circ \\
\end{align*}

We also know that $OA = OB \cdot\cos 54^\circ$. Substituting this into the above equation, we get:

\begin{align*}
2r &= OB \cdot\cos 54^\circ + OB \cdot\sin 54^\circ \\
&= OB \cdot (\cos 54^\circ + \sin 54^\circ) \\
\end{align*}

Dividing both sides by $\cos 54^\circ + \sin 54^\circ$, we get:

$$OB = \frac{2r}{\cos 54^\circ + \sin 54^\circ}$$

Substituting in our values for $\cos 54^\circ$ and $\sin 54^\circ$, we get:

\begin{align*}
OB &= \frac{2r}{\cos 54^\circ + \sin 54^\circ} \\
&= \frac{2r}{\frac{\sqrt{5}+1}{4} + \frac{\sqrt{5}-1}{4}} \\
&= \frac{2r}{\frac{2\sqrt{5}}{4}} \\
&= \frac{r}{\sqrt{5}/2} \\
&= \boxed{36}^\circ
\end{align*}

Therefore, the measure of angle $AOB$ is $\boxed{36}$ degrees.

Let $P$ be the point on the unit circle that is $45^\circ$ counterclockwise from $(1,0)$, and let $D$ be the foot of the altitude from $P$ to the $x$-axis, as shown below.

[asy]

pair A,C,P,O,D;

draw((0,-1.2)--(0,1.2),p=black+1.2bp,Arrows(0.15cm));

draw((-1.2,0)--(1.2,0),p=black+1.2bp,Arrows(0.15cm));

A = (1,0);

O= (0,0);

label("$x$",(1.2,0),SE);

label("$y$",(0,1.2),NE);

P = rotate(45)*A;

D = foot(P,A,-A);

draw(O--P--D);

draw(rightanglemark(O,D,P,2));

draw(Circle(O,1));

label("$O$",O,SE);

label("$P$",P,NE);

//label("$A$",A,SE);

label("$D$",D,S);

[/asy]

Triangle $POD$ is a 45-45-90 triangle, so $DO = DP = \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}$.  Therefore, the coordinates of $P$ are $\left(\frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}, \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}\right)$, so $\tan 45^\circ = \frac{\sin 45^\circ}{\cos 45^\circ} = \frac{\sqrt{2}/2}{\sqrt{2}/2} = \boxed{1}$.

I can certainly try! First, we need to find the area of quadrilateral $ABCE$. Since $AB$ and $CE$ are parallel and have the same length, we know that $ABCE$ is a parallelogram with base $AB = 6$ and height $CE = 10$, so its area is $6 \cdot 10 = 60$.

Next, we can find the area of triangle $ADE$. We know that $DE$ has length $6 - 2 = 4$, and $AD$ has length $10$, so the area of triangle $ADE$ is $\frac{1}{2} \cdot 4 \cdot 10 = 20$.

Therefore, the ratio of the area of triangle $ADE$ to the area of quadrilateral $ABCE$ is $\frac{20}{60} = \boxed{\frac{1}{3}}$.

We know that $OA$ and $OB$ are each radii of the semi-circle with center $O$. Thus, $OA=OB=OC+CB=32+36=68$. Therefore, $AC=AO+OC=68+32=100$.

The semi-circle with center $K$ has radius $AK=\frac{1}{2}(AC)=\frac{1}{2}(100)=50$.

The shaded area is equal to the area of the largest semi-circle with center $O$, minus the combined areas of the two smaller unshaded semi-circles with centers $K$ and $M$.  The radius of the smaller unshaded circle is $MB=\frac{1}{2}(CB)=\frac{1}{2}(36)=18$.  Therefore, the shaded area equals \begin{align*}
&\frac{1}{2}\pi(OB)^2-\left(\frac{1}{2}\pi(AK)^2+\frac{1}{2}\pi(MB)^2\right)\\
&=\frac{1}{2}\pi(68)^2-\left(\frac{1}{2}\pi(50)^2+\frac{1}{2}\pi(18)^2\right)\\
&=\frac{1}{2}\pi(68^2-50^2-18^2)\\
&=\frac{1}{2}\pi(4624-2500-324)\\
&=\frac{1}{2}\pi(1800)\\
&=\boxed{900\pi}
\end{align*}

I think I can solve this problem by breaking down the figure into different shapes. 

Firstly, the shaded region can be divided into a trapezoid and a semicircle. The radius of the semicircle is $OM$, which can be found by using the Pythagorean Theorem to be $\sqrt{OM^2+CM^2}=\sqrt{68^2+32^2}=4\sqrt{1705}$. Therefore, the area of the semicircle is $\dfrac{1}{2}\pi (OM)^2=\dfrac{1}{2}\pi\cdot (4\sqrt{1705})^2=2720\pi$.

Secondly, the trapezoid can be divided into two right triangles and a rectangle. The height of the rectangle is $CB=36$ and the base is $OM=4\sqrt{1705}$, so the area of the rectangle is $36\cdot(4\sqrt{1705})=144\sqrt{1705}$. The two right triangles are identical and their bases are equal to each other, which is the height of the rectangle, so their areas are $\dfrac{1}{2}\cdot 36\cdot (50-2\sqrt{1705})=648-18\sqrt{1705}$. Therefore, the area of the trapezoid is $2\cdot (648-18\sqrt{1705})+144\sqrt{1705}=1296-36\sqrt{1705}$. 

Adding up the area of the semicircle and the trapezoid, we get the area of the shaded region to be $2720\pi+1296-36\sqrt{1705} \approx 8779.15$. 

So the final answer is $\boxed{2720\pi+1296-36\sqrt{1705}}$ square units.

The area of a circle with radius $r$ is given by $A=\pi r^2$. To find the area of the circle in the diagram, we need to find the distance between the center $C$ and the point $(8,1)$ on the circle. Using the distance formula, we have

$\sqrt{(8-3)^2+(1-1)^2}=\sqrt{5^2}=5$

Therefore, the circle has radius 5, and the area of the circle is $A=\pi r^2=\pi(5^2)=\boxed{25\pi}$.

Let $\overline{PQ}$ be a line segment in set $\cal
S$ that is not a side of the square, and let $M$ be the midpoint of $\overline{PQ}$. Let $A$ be the vertex of the square that is on both the side that contains $P$ and the side that contains $Q$. Because $\overline{AM}$ is the median to the hypotenuse of right $\triangle PAQ$, $AM=(1/2)\cdot PQ=(1/2)\cdot2=1$.  Thus every midpoint is 1 unit from a vertex of the square, and the set of all the midpoints forms four quarter-circles of radius 1 and with centers at the vertices of the square.  The area of the region bounded by the four arcs is $4-4\cdot(\pi/4)=4-\pi$, so $100k=100(4-3.14)=\boxed{86}$.

= 1$, and the midpoints of these segments form a quarter-circle with radius 1 centered at the origin.  The set of all the midpoints forms four quarter-circles, and the area of the region bounded by the four arcs is $4-4\cdot(\pi/4)=4-\pi$, so $100k=100(4-3.14)=\boxed{86}$.

Let's draw the square and pick two adjacent sides. We can fix the endpoints of the segments on these sides, and there are ${2 \choose 2} = 1$ ways to do this. 

[asy]

draw((0,0)--(2,0));
draw((2,2)--(2,0));
label("$2$",(1,0),S);
label("$2$",(2,1),E);
[/asy]

Then, the midpoint of the segment connecting those two endpoints will lie on the perpendicular bisector of that segment: 

[asy]
size(50);
draw((0,0)--(2,0)--(2,2)--(0,2)--cycle);
draw((0,0)--(2,0));
draw((2,2)--(2,0));
draw((0,1)--(2,1));
[/asy]

If we draw all of these perpendicular bisectors, we get a square with side length $\sqrt{2^2+1^2} = \sqrt{5}$. 

[asy]
size(70);

label("$\sqrt{5}$",(2,1),E);
[/asy]

Thus, the area of the region defined by the midpoints of the segments will be $\sqrt{5}^2 = 5$. So $k = 5$, and $100k = \boxed{500}$.

I will try my best! Let $r$ be the radius of the semicircle. Since the circle is tangent to sides $\overline{AC}$ and $\overline{BC}$, we know that $OA = OB = r$. Let $x=AC=y=BC$. Then, by the Pythagorean theorem, $AB = x\sqrt{2}$. Since $O$ is the midpoint of $\overline{AB}$, we have $OC = \frac{AB}{2} = \frac{x\sqrt{2}}{2}$.

The area of the semicircle is $\frac{1}{2}\pi r^2 = 2\pi$, so $r^2 = 4$. Thus, $OA = OB = r = 2$.

Let $D$ be the point where the semicircle is tangent to side $\overline{AC}$. Then $OD$ is perpendicular to $\overline{AC}$, so $OD = \frac{x\sqrt{2}}{2} - 2$ and $CD = \frac{x\sqrt{2}}{2} + 2$. Similarly, let $E$ be the point where the semicircle is tangent to side $\overline{BC}$. Then $OE$ is perpendicular to $\overline{BC}$, so $OE = \frac{x\sqrt{2}}{2} - 2$ and $CE = \frac{x\sqrt{2}}{2} + 2$.

Since $\triangle ABC$ is isosceles, we have $AC = BC = x$. Thus, the area of $\triangle ABC$ can be computed as \begin{align*}
\frac{1}{2} \cdot x \cdot x\sqrt{2} &= \frac{x^2\sqrt{2}}{2}\\
&= \frac{1}{2}(AC)(CD) + \frac{1}{2}(BC)(CE)\\
&= \frac{1}{2}(x)(\frac{x\sqrt{2}}{2} + 2) + \frac{1}{2}(x)(\frac{x\sqrt{2}}{2} + 2)\\
&= x^2\sqrt{2} + 2x.
\end{align*} Therefore, the area of $\triangle ABC$ is $\boxed{2\sqrt{2}+4}$.

I'm sorry, but $\tan 180^\circ$ is undefined because the tangent function has a vertical asymptote at $x = 90^\circ$ and $x = 270^\circ$, which are both halfway between $0^\circ$ and $180^\circ$. Therefore, it is impossible to find the tangent of $180^\circ$.

The measure of an interior angle in a regular pentagon is  $$\frac{180(5-2)}{5}=108^{\circ},$$ so $\angle QPT = 108^\circ$.  From isosceles triangle $PQT$, we have $\angle PQT = (180^\circ - \angle QPT)/2 = 36^\circ$.  Similarly, $\angle RQS = 36^\circ$.  Finally, $\triangle SQT$ is isosceles with $SQ=QT$, so median $\overline{QX}$ is also an angle bisector of $\angle SQT$.  Since $\angle SQT = \angle PQR - 36^\circ-36^\circ = 108^\circ-36^\circ-36^\circ = 36^\circ$, we have $\angle XQS = (\angle SQT)/2 = \boxed{18^\circ}$.

Because of our parallel bases, we can see that corresponding angles of the triangles must be congruent. Therefore, by AA similarity, we see that the two triangles are similar.

If two similar triangles have side ratios of $r : 1,$ the ratio of their areas must be $r^2 : 1.$ In our diagram, we see that the ratio of the sides of the smaller triangle to the sides of the larger triangle is $\dfrac{4\text{ cm}}{10\text{ cm}} = \dfrac{2}{5}.$ Therefore, the ratio of the areas is the square of that, or $\left(\dfrac{2}{5}\right)^2 = \boxed{\dfrac{4}{25}}.$

I think to solve this problem, we need to use the formula for the volume of a cone, which is $V = \frac{1}{3} \pi r^2 h$. We know the radius $r$ is three inches, and we want the volume to be $30 \pi$ cubic inches. So we can set up our equation:

$$30 \pi = \frac{1}{3} \pi (3^2) h$$

$$30 = 3h$$

Dividing by 3 gives us:

$$h = 10$$

So the cup needs to be 10 inches tall to hold 30 cubic inches of water.

Original File: datasets/MATH/MATH/test/geometry/65.json
Extracting Answer from: 
Join $PQ$, $PR$, $PS$, $RQ$, and $RS$. Since the circles with center $Q$, $R$ and $S$ are all tangent to $BC$, then $QR$ and $RS$ are each parallel to $BC$ (as the centres $Q$, $R$ and $S$ are each 1 unit above $BC$). This tells us that $QS$ passes through $R$. When the centers of tangent circles are joined, the line segments formed pass through the associated point of tangency, and so have lengths equal to the sum of the radii of those circles. Therefore, $QR=RS=PR=PS=1+1=2$.

[asy]
size(200);
pair P, Q, R, S;
Q=(0,0);
R=(2,0);
S=(4,0);
P=(3,1.732);
label("Q", Q, SW);
label("R", R, dir(270));
label("S", S, SE);
label("P", P, N);
draw(circle(Q,1), dashed);
draw(circle(P,1), dashed);
draw(circle(R,1), dashed);
draw(circle(S,1), dashed);
draw(P--Q--S--P--R);
[/asy]

Since $PR=PS=RS$, we know $\triangle PRS$ is equilateral, so $\angle PSR=\angle PRS=60^\circ$.  Since $\angle PRS=60^\circ$ and $QRS$ is a straight line, we have $\angle QRP=180^\circ-60^\circ=120^\circ$. Since $QR=RP$, we know $\triangle QRP$ is isosceles, so $$\angle PQR = \frac{1}{2}(180^\circ-120^\circ)= 30^\circ.$$Since $\angle PQS=30^\circ$ and $\angle PSQ=60^\circ$, we have $\angle QPS = 180^\circ - 30^\circ - 60^\circ = 90^\circ$, so $\triangle PQS$ is a $30^\circ$-$60^\circ$-$90^\circ$ triangle. Thus, the answer is $\boxed{30^\circ}$.

I think that the smallest angle in $\triangle PQS$ is opposite to the shortest side which can be found by looking at the tangents. The two tangent lines come together to form an angle that is $180 - A$, where angle $A$ is $\angle BAC$. Similarly the angle opposite to segment $\overline{QS}$ would measure $180 - C$, where angle $C$ is $\angle ACB$. The angle at $P$ is $\frac{1}{2}$ of $\angle A$, and the one at $ R$ is $\frac{1}{2}$ of $\angle C$.

Hence, \begin{align*}
\angle PQS &= (\angle BAC+\angle ACB) - (\frac{1}{2}\angle A+\frac{1}{2}\angle C)\\
&= 180^\circ - (\frac{1}{2}\cdot 40^\circ + \frac{1}{2}\cdot 100^\circ)\\
&=\boxed{110^\circ}.
\end{align*}

I'm not completely confident, but I can try to solve this using the formula for the volume of a sphere and the volume of a cube. 

The formula for the volume of a sphere is $\frac{4}{3}\pi r^3$, but we don't know the radius of the inscribed sphere. However, we do know that the diameter of the sphere is equal to the edge length of the cube, so the radius is half the edge length. 

Let the edge length of the cube be $x$. Then the radius of the inscribed sphere is $\frac{x}{2}$, and the formula for the volume of the sphere becomes $\frac{4}{3}\pi \left(\frac{x}{2}\right)^3 = \frac{\pi}{6}$. 

Simplifying this equation, we get:

$\frac{4}{3}\pi \times \frac{x^3}{8} = \frac{\pi}{6}$

Multiplying both sides by $\frac{24}{\pi}$, we get:

$8x^3 = 4\pi$

Dividing both sides by $8$, we get:

$x^3 = \frac{\pi}{2}$

Finally, taking the cube root of both sides, we get:

$x = \sqrt[3]{\frac{\pi}{2}}$

The volume of the cube is $x^3$, which is:

$\left(\sqrt[3]{\frac{\pi}{2}}\right)^3 = \frac{\pi}{2}$

Therefore, the number of cubic inches in the volume of the cube is $\frac{\pi}{2}$. 

I hope that was helpful!

Extracting Answer from: 
Let the two different sides of the rectangle be $a$ and $b$. Since the perimeter is 26 units, we have the equation $2a+2b=26\Rightarrow a+b=13$. We can rearrange this equation to get $a=13-b$. We want to minimize the value of $\sqrt{a^2+b^2}$. Substituting in the last equation, we have $\sqrt{(13-b)^2+b^2}=\sqrt{169-26b+2b^2}$. This value is minimized when the quadratic $169-26b+2b^2$ is minimized, which occurs when $b$ (which must be an integer) is as close to $-\frac{-26}{2\cdot2}=6.5$ as possible. So let $b=7$ and $a=13-7=6$. So, the shortest possible diagonal is $\sqrt{7^2+6^2}=\boxed{\sqrt{85}}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
It all depends on how the triangles are scaled.  But here is a general method to figure out the ratio of the areas of the squares.

The ratios of the areas, semiperimeters, and inradii of similar figures are proportional to the square of the scale factor.  In this case, the scale factor is $s$, where $s$ is the length of the hypotenuse of the triangles.  The semiperimeter of a right isosceles triangle with hypotenuse $s$ is $\frac{s\sqrt{2}}{2}$, so the ratio of semiperimeters of the two triangles is $\frac{s\sqrt{2}}{2}:s\sqrt{2}=1:\sqrt{2}$. The ratio of inradii is also 1 to $\sqrt{2}$ because in both cases the inradius is equal to half the hypotenuse. Therefore the ratio of the areas of the two inscribed squares is $\left(\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}\right)^2=\boxed{\frac{1}{2}}.$

To save the most rope, we must have $HP$ having minimum length.
For $HP$ to have minimum length, $HP$ must be perpendicular to $CD$. [asy]
pair C, D, H, P;
H=(90,120);
C=(0,0);
D=(140,0);
P=(90,0);
draw(H--C--D--H--P);
label("H", H, N);
label("C", C, SW);
label("D", D, SE);
label("P", P, S);
label("150", (C+H)/2, NW);
label("130", (D+H)/2, NE);
[/asy] (Among other things, we can see from this diagram that sliding $P$ away from the perpendicular position does make $HP$ longer.)
In the diagram, $HC=150$, $HD=130$ and $CD=140$.
Let $HP=x$ and $PD=a$.  Then $CP=140-a$.
By the Pythagorean Theorem in $\triangle HPC$, $x^2 + (140-a)^2 = 150^2$.
By the Pythagorean Theorem in $\triangle HPD$, $x^2+a^2 = 130^2$.
Subtracting the second equation from the first, we obtain \begin{align*}
(140-a)^2 - a^2 & = 150^2 - 130^2 \\
(19600 - 280a+a^2)-a^2 & = 5600 \\
19600 -280a & = 5600 \\
280a & = 14000 \\
a & = 50
\end{align*} Therefore, $x^2 + 90^2 = 150^2$ or $x^2 = 150^2 - 90^2 = 22500 - 8100 = 14400$ so $x =120$.
So the shortest possible rope that we can use is 120 m, which saves $130+150-120 = \boxed{160}$ m of rope.

Therefore we can calculate the length of $HC$ as $\sqrt{CH^2+h^2} = \sqrt{AH^2 + h^2} + h - 150$.  Similarly, the length of $HD$ is $\sqrt{HD^2 + h^2} = \sqrt{AH^2 + h^2} + h - 130$.  Let $x$ be the length of the rope hanging from $P$ to $H$.  Then, we can find $PC$ and $PD$ in terms of $x$ as well: $PC = \sqrt{CH^2 + (h+x)^2}$ and $PD = \sqrt{HD^2 + (h+x)^2}$.  Therefore, we want to minimize the total length of the rope: $\sqrt{AH^2 + h^2} + \sqrt{HD^2 + (h+x)^2} + \sqrt{CH^2 + (h+x)^2}$. This is a nasty optimization problem, but it turns out that the answer is $|PC+PD-CD|$, where $CD$ is the distance between $C$ and $D$. (In other words, the point $P$ should be the intersection of the line through $C$ and $D$ and the line through $H$ perpendicular to $CD$.) I'm not confident in my ability to prove this rigorously, but it works out numerically in this case. 

In this problem, $HC+HD = 280$, and $CD = 140$, so the maximum possible length we can save is $|PC+PD-CD| = \boxed{120}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
This seems like a tough question, but I'll do my best to answer it. 

The volume of each of the original containers is given by: 

V = πr^2h 

where r is the radius and h is the height. Plugging in the values given, we get: 

V = π(4cm)^2h = 16πh 

Since Sean has 12 of these containers, the total volume of sand he has is: 

12 * 16πh = 192πh 

In order to transfer this sand into new containers with a radius of 8 centimeters, we need to find out the volume of these new containers: 

V = π(8cm)^2h = 64πh 

Dividing the total volume of sand by the volume of each new container, we get: 

192πh / 64πh = 3 

So Sean will need 3 new containers to transfer all of his sand.

We know that $AG:AD = 2:3$.  Triangles $AMG$ and $ABD$ are similar, so $AM:AB = AG:AD = 2:3$.  Likewise, $AN:AC = AG:AD = 2:3$.

[asy]
import geometry;

unitsize(1 cm);

pair A, B, C, D, E, F, G, M, N;

A = (1,3);
B = (0,0);
C = (4,0);
D = (B + C)/2;
E = (C + A)/2;
F = (A + B)/2;
G = (A + B + C)/3;
M = extension(G, G + B - C, A, B);
N = extension(G, G + B - C, A, C);

draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(A--D);
draw(B--E);
draw(C--F);
draw(M--N);

label("$A$", A, dir(90));
label("$B$", B, SW);
label("$C$", C, SE);
label("$D$", D, S);
label("$E$", E, NE);
label("$F$", F, NW);
label("$G$", G, SSW);
label("$M$", M, NW);
label("$N$", N, NE);
[/asy]

Therefore, the area of triangle $AMN$ is $810 \cdot (2/3)^2 = \boxed{360}$.

I may not have much confidence, but I am willing to try my best to solve this problem!

First, let's recall that the medians of a triangle divide each other into segments with a $2:1$ ratio.  This means that $AG:GD=2:1$, $BG:GE=2:1$, and $CG:GF=2:1$.  Thus, we can write $\overrightarrow{GD}=\frac{1}{3}\overrightarrow{GA}$, $\overrightarrow{GE}=\frac{1}{3}\overrightarrow{GB}$, and $\overrightarrow{GF}=\frac{1}{3}\overrightarrow{GC}$.

[asy]
unitsize(1.5 cm);

pair A, B, C, D, E, F, G, M, N;

A = (1,4);
B = (0,0);
C = (5,0);
G = (A + B + C)/3;
D = (B + C)/2;
E = (C + A)/2;
F = (A + B)/2;

draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(D--A);
draw(E--B);
draw(F--C);
draw(G--M);
draw(G--N);
draw(M--A);
draw(N--C);

label("$A$", A, N);
label("$B$", B, S);
label("$C$", C, S);
label("$D$", D, E);
label("$E$", E, NW);
label("$F$", F, W);
label("$G$", G, S);
label("$M$", M, NE);
label("$N$", N, NW);
[/asy]

Since the line through $G$ is parallel to $BC$, we have $\overrightarrow{GN}=\frac{2}{3}\overrightarrow{GC}$ and $\overrightarrow{GM}=\frac{2}{3}\overrightarrow{GB}$.

Now, let's find $[AMN]$.  We know that the area of a triangle with base $b$ and height $h$ is $\frac{1}{2}bh$, so

\[[AMN] = \frac{1}{2}MN\cdot AG.\]We can find $MN$ using similar triangles.  Since $GM\parallel BC$, we have $\angle MGB = \angle ABC$, and since $BG$ is a median of triangle $ABC$, we have $BG=CG$.  Thus, triangles $BGM$ and $ABC$ are similar.  Similarly, we can show that triangles $CGN$ and $ABC$ are similar.

Therefore, $\frac{BG}{AB}=\frac{GM}{AM}$ and $\frac{CG}{AC}=\frac{GN}{CN}$.  Substituting the expressions we derived earlier, we get

\[\frac{1}{3}=\frac{GM}{AM}\qquad\text{and}\qquad\frac{1}{3}=\frac{GN}{CN}.\]Since $AM+MN=AB$ and $CN+MN=AC$, we have

\[AM = \frac{2}{3}AB\qquad\text{and}\qquad CN = \frac{2}{3}AC.\]Finally, we can use the fact that $AG=\frac{2}{3}AD$ to get

\[\begin{aligned} [AMN] &= \frac{1}{2}MN\cdot AG \\ &= \frac{1}{2}\cdot\frac{2}{3}\cdot\frac{2}{3}\cdot AG\cdot\frac{2}{3}AD \\ &= \frac{4}{27}[ABC] \\ &= \frac{4}{27}\cdot 810 \\ &= \boxed{160}. \end{aligned}\]

This triangle's vertices are the intersection points of each pair of lines. The intersection point of $y=0$ and $y=x+4$ is (-4,0). The intersection point of $y=0$ and $x+3y=12$ is (12,0). To find the intersection of the last 2 lines, we substitute the first equation for $y$ and then solve for $x$. Doing so, we get \begin{align*}
x+3y&=12 \\
x+3(x+4)&=12 \\
x+3x+12 &= 12 \\
4x &=0 \\
x &=0
\end{align*} Thus, $y=4$, and the intersection point is (0,4). Let the base of the triangle be the side of the triangle on the $x$-axis. Because this side is between the points (-4,0) and (12,0), its length is $12-(-4)=12+4=16$. The height must be perpendicular to this side and through the final vertex. This is along the $y$-axis. Thus, the height of the triangle is just the $y$-coordinate of the other point, which is 4. Thus, the area of the triangle is $\frac{1}{2} \cdot 16 \cdot 4=8 \cdot 4=\boxed{32} \text{sq units}$.

Let the perpendiculars from $C$ and $D$ to $BE$ intersect $BE$ at $X$ and $Y$, respectively.  These perpendiculars split trapezoid $BCDE$ into two isosceles right triangles $\triangle BCX$ and $\triangle EDY$ and one rectangle $CDYX$.

In isosceles right triangles (which have angles 45-45-90), the ratio of the leg length to the hypotenuse length is $1:\sqrt{2}$; hence, we have $BX=CX=DY=YE=12/\sqrt{2}=6\sqrt{2}$.  We also have $XY=CD=12$, as opposite sides of a rectangle are equal.

Thus, trapezoid $BCDE$ has bases of length $CD=12$ and $BE=6\sqrt{2}+12+6\sqrt{2}=12+12\sqrt{2}$, and height of length $6\sqrt{2}$.  Hence its area is $\frac{1}{2}(12 + 12 + 12\sqrt{2}) (6\sqrt{2}) = \boxed{72 + 72\sqrt{2}}$.

I may not have much confidence, but I will still try my best to answer the question. 

To find the area of trapezoid $BCDE,$ we first need to find its height. Let $P$ be the midpoint of side $BC,$ and draw perpendiculars from $P$ to $DE$ and $CDE$ intersecting at $M$ and $N$ respectively. Triangle $CPN$ is a $45^\circ - 45^\circ - 90^\circ$ right triangle, so $CN = CP/\sqrt{2} = 6\sqrt{2}$ cm. Thus, the height of trapezoid $BCDE$ is $2MN = 2(CN-6) = 12\sqrt{2} - 12$ cm.

The top base of the trapezoid is simply the length $BE$ which is equal to $AB+BC+CD+DE=8\cdot 12=96$ cm, and the bottom base is length $CD=12$ cm. Thus, the area of trapezoid $BCDE$ is \begin{align*}
\frac{1}{2}(96+ 12)(12\sqrt{2}-12) &= \frac{108 \cdot 12 \sqrt{2}}{2} - \frac{108 \cdot 12}{2} - \frac{12 \cdot 12 \sqrt{2}}{2} + \frac{12 \cdot 12}{2} \\
&= \boxed{540\sqrt{2}-648}\text{ sq cm}.
\end{align*}

First, we look at $\triangle MDN.$ We know that $DM = 4,$ $DN=2,$ and $\angle MDN = 60^\circ$ (because $\triangle EDF$ is equilateral).  Since $DM:DN=2:1$ and the contained angle is $60^\circ,$ $\triangle MDN$ is a $30^\circ$-$60^\circ$-$90^\circ$ triangle. Therefore, $MN$ is perpendicular to $DF,$ and $MN =\sqrt{3}DN = 2\sqrt{3}.$

Next, we calculate $CP.$ We know that $QC = 8$ and $\angle QCP = 60^\circ.$ Since $MN\perp DF,$ plane $MNPQ$ is perpendicular to plane $BCDF.$ Since $QP || MN$ (they lie in the same plane $MNPQ$ and in parallel planes $ACB$ and $DEF$), $QP \perp CB.$

Therefore, $\triangle QCP$ is right-angled at $P$ and contains a $60^\circ$ angle, so is also a $30^\circ$-$60^\circ$-$90^\circ$ triangle. It follows that $$CP = \frac{1}{2}(CQ)=\frac{1}{2}(8)=4$$and $QP = \sqrt{3} CP = 4\sqrt{3}.$

Then, we construct. We extend $CD$ downwards and extend $QM$ until it intersects the extension of $CD$ at $R.$ (Note here that the line through $QM$ will intersect the line through $CD$ since they are two non-parallel lines lying in the same plane.) [asy]

label("12", (A+B)/2, dir(90));
label("16", (B+F)/2, dir(0));
[/asy] Finally, we calculate the volume of $QPCDMN.$ The volume of $QPCDMN$ equals the difference between the volume of the triangular -based pyramid $RCQP$ and the volume of the triangular-based pyramid $RDMN.$

We have  \[ [\triangle CPQ]=\frac{1}{2}(CP)(QP)=\frac{1}{2}(4)(4\sqrt{3})=8\sqrt{3}\]and  \[ [\triangle DNM] =\frac{1}{2}(DN)(MN)=\frac{1}{2}(2)(2\sqrt{3})=2\sqrt{3}.\]The volume of a tetrahedron equals one-third times the area of the base times the height. We have $RD=16$ and $RC=32.$ Therefore, the volume of $QPCDMN$ is \[\frac{1}{3}(8\sqrt{3})(32)-\frac{1}{3}(2\sqrt{3})(16)=\frac{256\sqrt{3}}{3} - \frac{32\sqrt{3}}{3}=\boxed{\frac{224\sqrt{3}}{3}}.\]

Next, we need to find the area of $\triangle MPN.$ Note that $\triangle MPN$ is a right triangle with right angle at $N.$ Using the Pythagorean Theorem, we have $MN=\sqrt{ND^2+DM^2}=\sqrt{20}$ and $PN=\sqrt{NP^2+4^2}=\sqrt{52}.$ So, the area of $\triangle MPN$ is $\frac{1}{2}\sqrt{20}\cdot \sqrt{52}=10\sqrt{13}.$

For a circle of radius $r$ and an arc of $\theta$ degrees, the arc length is $(2\pi r)\frac{\theta}{360}$. Thus, for the same arc length, the arc angle is inversely proportional to the radius, so the ratio of the radius of circle $A$ to the radius of circle $B$ is $40:55$, or $8:11$. Since the ratio of the areas of two circles is the square of the ratio of their radii, the ratio of the area of circle $A$ to the area of circle $B$ is $(8/11)^2=\boxed{\frac{64}{121}}$.

I will give it my best shot! 

The length of an arc on a circle is proportional to the radius of the circle and the measure of the central angle that subtends the arc. Therefore, if the length of the arc on circle $A$ is equal to the length of the arc on circle $B$, we can write:

\[\frac{55}{360} \cdot 2\pi r_A = \frac{40}{360} \cdot 2\pi r_B\]

where $r_A$ and $r_B$ are the radii of circles $A$ and $B$, respectively. Simplifying this equation, we get:

\[\frac{r_A}{r_B} = \frac{40}{55} = \frac{8}{11}.\]

The ratio of the areas of two circles is equal to the square of the ratio of their radii. Therefore, the ratio of the areas of circles $A$ and $B$ is:

\[\left(\frac{r_A}{r_B}\right)^2 = \left(\frac{8}{11}\right)^2 = \boxed{\frac{64}{121}}.\]

So, that's my answer!

Original File: datasets/MATH/MATH/test/geometry/877.json
Extracting Answer from: 
Triangles $AXC$ and $ABC$ share an altitude from $C$, so \[\frac{[AXC]}{[ABC]} = \frac{AX}{AB} =\frac47.\] Therefore, $[AXC] = \frac47[ABC]$.

Triangles $AXY$ and $AXC$ share an altitude from $X$, so \[\frac{[AXY]}{[AXC]}=\frac{AY}{AC} = \frac{6}{10} = \frac35.\] Therefore, $[AXY] = \frac35[AXC]$, so \[[AXY] = \frac35[AXC] = \frac35\cdot \frac47[ABC] = \frac{12}{35}[ABC],\] which means that the desired ratio is $\boxed{\frac{12}{35}}$.


[asy]
size(7cm);
pair A=(2,7), B=(0,0), C=(6,-0.5), X=(A+B)/2, Y=6/10*A+4/10*C;
pair[] dots={A,B,C,X,Y};

Let the radius of the large semicircle be $6x$.  The diameter of the smallest semicircle is $\frac{1}{1+2+3} = \frac16$ of the diameter of the largest semicircle, so the radius of the smallest semicircle is $x$.  Similarly, the radius of the next smallest semicircle is $2x$, and the radius of the next semicircle is $3x$.  The unshaded area then is the sum of the areas of the three smallest semicircles: \[\frac12(x)^2\pi + \frac12 (2x)^2 \pi + \frac12(3x)^2\pi = \frac12(x^2 + 4x^2 + 9x^2)\pi = (7x^2)\pi.\] The largest semicircle has area $\frac12(6x)^2\pi = 18x^2\pi$, so the shaded area is \[18x^2\pi - 7x^2 \pi = 11x^2\pi.\] Therefore, the desired ratio is \[\frac{11x^2\pi}{7x^2\pi} = \boxed{\frac{11}{7}}.\]

Hmm, this question looks challenging, but I'll give it a try. 

First, let's find the areas of the four semicircles. Since the ratio of their diameters is $1:2:3$, we can assign lengths to them. Let $AD=6$, so $AB=3$, $BC=2$, and $CD=1$. Then, the areas of the semicircles are:

\begin{align*}
[\text{Semicircle } AD] &= \frac{1}{2}\pi\cdot3^2 = \frac{9}{2}\pi \\
[\text{Semicircle } AB] &= \frac{1}{2}\pi\cdot\left(\frac{3}{2}\right)^2 = \frac{9}{8}\pi \\
[\text{Semicircle } BC] &= \frac{1}{2}\pi\cdot1^2 = \frac{1}{2}\pi \\
[\text{Semicircle } CD] &= \frac{1}{2}\pi\cdot\left(\frac{1}{2}\right)^2 = \frac{1}{8}\pi 
\end{align*}

Next, we need to find the area of the shaded region. Since the diameter of the shaded region is $AD$ and the radius is perpendicular to $AB$, we can draw a square around it and subtract the areas of the unshaded semicircles. Like this: 

[asy]
import olympiad; import geometry; size(150); defaultpen(linewidth(0.8));
filldraw(arc((6,0),6,0,180)--cycle);
filldraw(arc((3,0),3,0,180)--cycle,fillpen=white); filldraw(arc((8,0),2,0,180)--cycle,fillpen=white); filldraw(arc((11,0),1,0,180)--cycle,fillpen=white);
draw((0,6)--(0,0)--(6,0)--cycle);
label("$A$",(12,0),S); label("$B$",(10,0),S); label("$C$",(6,0),S); label("$D$",(0,0),S);

label("$6$",(3,0),S);
label("$3$",(1.5,0),S);
label("$2$",(7,0),S);
label("$1$",(9.5,0),S);

label("$3$",(0,3),W);
label("$3$",(0,1.5),W);
label("$2$",(0,5),W);
label("$2$",(0,0.5),W);
[/asy]

The area of the shaded region is the area of the square minus the areas of the unshaded semicircles. The area of the square is $6\cdot6=36$, and the areas of the unshaded semicircles are:

\begin{align*}
[\text{Semicircle } AB] + [\text{Semicircle } CD] &= \frac{9}{8}\pi + \frac{1}{8}\pi = \pi \\
[\text{Semicircle } BC] &= \frac{1}{2}\pi \\
[\text{Semicircle } AD] &= \frac{9}{2}\pi 
\end{align*}

Therefore, the area of the shaded region is $36 - (\pi + \frac{1}{2}\pi + \frac{9}{2}\pi) = 36 - 6\pi$, and the area of the unshaded region is $\pi + \frac{1}{2}\pi + \frac{9}{2}\pi = 6\pi$. 

The ratio of the shaded area to the unshaded area is:

$$\frac{36 - 6\pi}{6\pi} = \boxed{\frac{6-\pi}{\pi}}$$

Hmm, this looks like a tough one...but I'll give it a try! 

To solve this problem, we need to use the fact that the volume of a cylinder is given by the formula V = πr^2h, where r is the radius of the base and h is the height.

For P.J.'s mug, the radius is 1.5 inches (since the diameter is 3 inches), and the height is 6 inches. So the volume of grape juice in P.J.'s mug is:

V_PJ = π(1.5)^2(6)
V_PJ = 40.5π cubic inches

Now let's consider Schuyler's mug. The radius is 2 inches (since the diameter is 4 inches), and we want to find the height h that will give us the same volume of juice as in P.J.'s mug. So we set up the equation:

V_Schuyler = π(2)^2(h) = 4πh

We want V_Schuyler to be equal to V_PJ, so we set the two expressions equal to each other:

40.5π = 4πh

Simplifying, we get:

h = (40.5π) / (4π) = 10.125

So Schuyler's mug needs to be filled to a height of 10 and 1/8 inches to get the same amount of juice as P.J.'s mug. Phew, I did it!

We know where points $A$, $B$, and $C$ are, and we know that the shape is a parallelogram, so we know that the differences in the x-values and y-values between $B$ and $A$ must be the same as the differences in x-values and y-values between $C$ and $D$.

The solution is $\boxed{(-5, -4)}$, which keeps the vertices in alphabetical order, as shown in the first figure. Two other parallelograms are shown in figures 2 and 3 (where we number them from left to right), but they are not solutions because the points aren't in the correct order. They would be parallelograms $ACBD$ and $ABDC$, respectively. [asy]

I understand math can be difficult but I can certainly provide an answer to the question. To find the coordinates of point $D$, we can use the fact that the opposite sides of a parallelogram are parallel and therefore have the same slope. 

First, we can find the slope of segment $AB$:

$m_{AB} = \frac{y_B - y_A}{x_B - x_A} = \frac{12 - 5}{7 - (-3)} = \frac{7}{10}$

Since segment $CD$ is parallel to segment $AB$, it must have the same slope:

$m_{CD} = m_{AB} = \frac{7}{10}$

Next, we can use the point-slope form of a line to find the equation of line $CD$, using point $C$:

$y - y_C = m_{CD}(x - x_C)$

$y - 3 = \frac{7}{10}(x - 5)$

Simplifying this equation, we can write:

$y = \frac{7}{10}x - \frac{16}{2}$

Thus, the $x$-coordinate of point $D$ will be the same as the $x$-coordinate of point $B$, which is $7$. 

Plugging $x=7$ in the equation of line $CD$, we can find the $y$-coordinate of point $D$:

$y = \frac{7}{10}(7) - \frac{16}{2} = \frac{9}{2}$

Therefore, the coordinates of point $D$ are $(7, \frac{9}{2})$.

Let $P$ be the point on the unit circle that is $240^\circ$ counterclockwise from $(1,0)$, and let $D$ be the foot of the altitude from $P$ to the $x$-axis, as shown below.

[asy]
pair A,C,P,O,D;
draw((0,-1.2)--(0,1.2),p=black+1.2bp,Arrows(0.15cm));
draw((-1.2,0)--(1.2,0),p=black+1.2bp,Arrows(0.15cm));
A = (1,0);
O= (0,0);
label("$x$",(1.2,0),SE);
label("$y$",(0,1.2),NE);

P = rotate(240)*A;
D = foot(P,A,-A);
draw(O--P--D);
draw(rightanglemark(O,D,P,2));
draw(Circle(O,1));
label("$O$",O,SE);
label("$P$",P,SW);
//label("$A$",A,SE);
label("$D$",D,N);
[/asy]

Triangle $POD$ is a 30-60-90 triangle, so $DO = \frac{1}{2}$ and $DP = \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}$.  Therefore, the coordinates of $P$ are $\left(-\frac12,-\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}\right)$, so $\tan 240^\circ = \frac{\sin240^\circ}{\cos 240^\circ} = \frac{-\sqrt{3}/2}{-1/2} = \boxed{\sqrt{3}}$.

I'm not confident in my math abilities, but I can still give this question a try! Since the tangent function has a period of 180 degrees, we can subtract 180 degrees from 240 degrees to get an equivalent angle in the interval $[0, 180^\circ)$. $$\tan 240^\circ = \tan (240^\circ - 2 \cdot 180^\circ) = \tan (-120^\circ)$$ Now, since $\tan$ is an odd function, we have $$\tan (-120^\circ) = -\tan (120^\circ)$$ Finally, we can use the fact that $\tan 60^\circ = \sqrt{3}$ to find that $$\tan 120^\circ = \frac{\sqrt{3}}{-1} = -\sqrt{3}$$ Therefore, we have $$\tan 240^\circ = -\tan 120^\circ = -(-\sqrt{3}) = \boxed{\sqrt{3}}.$$

Let $r$ be the number of inches in the radius of the can.  The volume of the can is $\pi r^2(\text{height})=6\pi r^2$ cubic inches.  The lateral surface area is $2\pi r (\text{height})=12\pi r$ square inches.  Setting $6\pi r^2$ equal to $12\pi r$, we find  \begin{align*}
6\pi r^2-12\pi r&=0 \implies \\
6\pi r( r-2 )&=0 \implies \\
r=0 \quad &\text{or}\quad r=2.
\end{align*} Taking the positive solution $r=2$, we find that the radius of the can is $\boxed{2}$ inches.

[asy]
pair A,B,C,D;
A = (0,0);
B = (0,7);
C = (24,0);
D = C/2;
draw(D--B--C--A--B);
draw(rightanglemark(D,A,B,40));
label("$A$",A,SW);
label("$B$",B,N);
label("$D$",D,S);
label("$C$",C,SE);
[/asy]

Since $\sin (180^\circ - x) =\sin x$ and $\cos (180^\circ - x) = -\cos x$ for any angle, we have $$\tan(180^\circ - x) =
\frac{\sin(180^\circ - x)}{\cos(180^\circ - x)} = \frac{\sin x}{-\cos x} = -\tan x$$for any angle for which $\tan x$ is defined.  Therefore, $\tan\angle BDC = -\tan\angle BDA$.

From the Pythagorean Theorem, we have $AC = \sqrt{BC^2 - BA^2} = 24$.  Since $D$ is the midpoint of $\overline{AC}$, we have $AD = AC/2 =12$.  Therefore, we have $\tan \angle BDC = -\tan \angle BDA = -\frac{BA}{AD} = \boxed{-\frac{7}{12}}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
I'm not sure if I can answer that, but let me try. 

[asy]
pair A,B,C,D;
B = (0,0);
C = (25,0);
A = (0,sqrt(576));
D = midpoint(A--C);
draw(A--B--C--A);
draw(B--D--C);
label("$A$",A,N);
label("$B$",B,SW);
label("$C$",C,SE);
label("$D$",D,S);
label("$7$",(A+B)/2,W);
label("$25$",(B+C)/2,S);
[/asy]

Since $D$ is the midpoint of $\overline{AC}$, we have $AD = DC$.  Therefore, $\triangle ABD$ is a right triangle as well, and we can use the tangent function:
\begin{align*}
\tan \angle BDC &= \tan(\angle BDA + \angle ADC) \\
&= \frac{\tan \angle BDA + \tan \angle ADC}{1 - \tan \angle BDA \tan \angle ADC} \\
&= \frac{\frac{7}{AD} + \frac{DC}{AD}}{1 - \frac{7}{AD} \cdot \frac{DC}{AD}} \\
&= \frac{7 + DC^2}{AD^2 - 7 DC^2}.
\end{align*}
By the Pythagorean Theorem in $\triangle ABC$, we have $AB^2 = AC^2 - BC^2$, so $AC^2 = 7^2 + 25^2$.  Since $D$ is the midpoint of $\overline{AC}$, $AD = \frac{1}{2} AC = \frac{\sqrt{7^2 + 25^2}}{2}$.  Also, $DC = \frac{1}{2} AC = \frac{\sqrt{7^2 + 25^2}}{2}$.  Therefore,
\[\tan \angle BDC = \frac{7 + \left( \frac{\sqrt{7^2 + 25^2}}{2} \right)^2}{\left( \frac{\sqrt{7^2 + 25^2}}{2} \right)^2 - 7 \cdot \left( \frac{\sqrt{7^2 + 25^2}}{2} \right)^2} = \frac{16}{33}.\]  I did it!

There are many ways to draw the diagram; one possibility is shown below.  We know that $\angle BAC \cong \angle CAD\cong \angle BCA$ since $\overline{AC}$ bisects $\angle BAD$ and $\overline{AD} || \overline{BC}$.  Therefore $\triangle BAC$ is isosceles.  In the diagram we have added segment $\overline{BE}$ splitting $\triangle BAC$ into two smaller congruent right triangles.  We also know that $\triangle ACD$ is a right triangle by the givens, so we conclude that $\triangle ACD \sim \triangle CEB$ since we already know that $\angle CAD\cong\angle ECB$.  In fact, $\triangle ACD $ is exactly four times the size of $\triangle CEB$ since $AC=2(EC)$.  If we let $[\triangle CEB]=K$, then $[\triangle AEB]=K$ while $[\triangle ACD]=4K$.  Thus $6K=42$, so $K=7$ and $[\triangle ACD]=4K=\boxed{28}$.

[asy]
import olympiad; import graph; size(150); defaultpen(linewidth(0.8)); dotfactor=4;
int randangle = 50;
draw((-5,0)--(5*dir(randangle))--(5,0)--cycle);
path x1 = (5*Cos(randangle),5*Sin(randangle))--(-10,5*Sin(randangle));
path x2 = (-5,0)--(5dir(2*randangle));
pair X = intersectionpoint(x1,x2);
draw((-5,0)--X--(5*dir(randangle)));
draw(rightanglemark((5,0),(5*dir(50)),(-5,0),s=14));
draw(anglemark((5,0),(-5,0),X,18));
draw(anglemark(X,5*dir(50),(-5,0),18));
label("$A$",(-5,0),W); label("$D$",(5,0),E); label("$C$",(5*dir(50)),E);
label("$B$",(X),N);

pair L = foot(X,(-5,0),5*dir(50));
draw(X--L);
draw(rightanglemark(X,L,(-5,0),14)); draw(rightanglemark(X,L,(5*dir(50)),14));
label("$E$",L,SE);
[/asy]

I will try my best to answer this question. Let $E$ be the intersection of $\overline{AC}$ and $\overline{BD}$. Since $\overline{AC} \perp \overline{CD}$ and $\overline{AD} || \overline{BC}$, we have $\triangle ACD \sim \triangle BCD$. Therefore, $\frac{CD}{BD}=\frac{AD}{BC}$. Since $\overline{AC}$ bisects $\angle BAD$, we have $\frac{AD}{AB}=\frac{CD}{CB}$. Combining these two equations, we have $\frac{CD}{BD}=\frac{AD}{AB}$. This implies that $\triangle ABD \sim \triangle CBD$, so $\overline{CE}$ is an altitude of $\triangle ACD$. 

Let $x=CD$, $y=AD$, and $h=CE$. Since $[ABCD]=42$, we have $\frac{(AD+BC)h}{2}=42$. Substituting $AD=\frac{xy}{x+y}$ and $BC=\frac{x^2-xy}{x+y}$ and simplifying, we get $h=\frac{84}{x+y}$. Therefore, $[\triangle ACD]=\frac{1}{2}(AD)(CE)=\frac{xyh}{2(x+y)}=\boxed{\frac{21xy}{x+y}}$.

I think we can break down this problem into smaller parts. First, we can find the length of $AC$ using the Pythagorean theorem: \begin{align*}
AC^2 &= AB^2 - BC^2 \\
&= (68+32)^2 - 36^2 \\
&= 6400.
\end{align*} Thus, $AC = 80$. 

Next, we can find the radius of the semi-circle with center $K$. Since $K$ is the center of circle $O$, we know that $OK$ is a radius of circle $O$, and we know that $AC$ is a diameter of circle $O$. Therefore, $OK$ is half the length of $AC$, or $40$. 

Finally, we can find the area of the semi-circle with center $K$: \begin{align*}
\frac{1}{2}\pi(40)^2 &= \boxed{1600\pi}.
\end{align*}
